201404	1788	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	President Evans.	92	9
201404	1789	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201404	1790	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	And thanks to the staff for a lot of hard work on the memos.	92	9
201404	1791	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	We have known for some time that our eventual policy normalization would be challenging.	92	9
201404	1792	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	As our discussion revealed just now, there are several ways to proceed.	92	9
201404	1793	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I expect that we will continue to narrow our proposals over the next several meetings until we arrive at a workable and effective consensus approach.	92	9
201404	1794	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I have just a couple of comments at this point.	92	9
201404	1795	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I think our monetary policy task is relatively straightforward.	92	9
201404	1796	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	We want to preserve our ability to adjust a short-term policy rate when it comes time to impose more restrictive financial conditions.	92	9
201404	1797	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	As Vice Chairman Dudley mentioned, this is really the most important objective, and I think most everybody around the table has echoed that.	92	9
201404	1798	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I personally believe that the time for tightening is not near.	92	9
201404	1799	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	My submission for the March Summary of Economic Projections had the first policy rate liftoff in the first quarter of 2016—if anybody didn’t already know that.	92	9
201404	1800	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	But of course, we do need to plan.	92	9
201404	1801	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	We have a variety of interest rates that we could employ:  the IOER rate, the overnight reverse repo rate, the fed funds rate or its next generation, the GC repo rate, and others.	92	9
201404	1802	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	As long as these candidate rates remain relevant for the pricing of other market interest rates in the US credit and intermediation process, any of them might work as the primary focus of our policy actions, it seems to me.	92	9
201404	1803	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I agree with President Lacker’s comment that the volatility of the funds rate and of the other rates are really of secondary importance relative to our policy objectives.	92	9
201404	1804	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	So until we have more discriminating evidence on the relative efficacy of the various approaches on the table, I remain relatively open-minded as to the best way to proceed on this front.	92	9
201404	1805	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	Now, that said, I found the briefing documents helpful for pointing out that some of these new tools could have a bearing on altering financial instability risk for the US economy.	92	9
201404	1806	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	Although the creation of overnight reverse repo assets—can we find a shorter way to describe that, please?—has some appealing properties for supplying much sought-after short-term safe- instrument assets, these new financial products may change the industry landscape, and maybe by a lot.	92	9
201404	1807	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I’m quite sympathetic to President Plosser’s point that we need to be thinking about the endpoint—others have said that, too—of where we want to be.	92	9
201404	1808	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	And once I started thinking about what the long-term landscape of the financial industry might be because of the introduction of these nice, safe assets, I couldn’t stop thinking about it, really.	92	9
201404	1809	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	And I don’t know how to disengage from that or finesse it.	92	9
201404	1810	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I mean, maybe we don’t need to have a firm opinion about that just to make a decision in September.	92	9
201404	1811	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	But I think we have to have that endpoint in mind.	92	9
201404	1812	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	It’s not clear to me how this will work.	92	9
201404	1813	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	For example, how would money market mutual funds that specialize in overnight reverse repo assets affect other money market fund assets and the credit instruments that are associated with them?	92	9
201404	1814	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	Maybe this is trivial and negligible.	92	9
201404	1815	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	That was sort of the commentary I got from the questions that I tried to pose.	92	9
201404	1816	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	But I was focusing on the longer term; I’m not sure we engaged on that quite enough.	92	9
201404	1817	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I would want strong assurances that no unintended credit crunch could emerge for any important nonfinancial enterprise.	92	9
201404	1818	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I think that as we try to normalize policy, we are going to want to make sure that we don’t somehow create some kind of mistake that affects the credit process.	92	9
201404	1819	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I’m also not sure how I feel about the possibility that our choice of monetary policy tools could significantly alter a large financial services industry segment.	92	9
201404	1820	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	Perhaps this industry evolution is inevitable, but it does seem a bit far removed from our normal monetary policymaking considerations, as well as the more immediate task at hand of ensuring our ability to raise short-term interest rates when the time comes.	92	9
201404	1821	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	There is a lot we need to understand about these choices.	92	9
201404	1822	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	But, again, I remain open-minded on the use of overnight reverse repo assets as an important element of our policy toolkit.	92	9
201404	1823	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	Now, regarding the seven questions, I am extremely pleased to say that I am in complete agreement with President Fisher.	92	9
201404	1824	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I don’t really intend to answer those questions.	92	9
201404	1825	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	[Laughter]	92	9
201404	1826	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I am open-minded.	92	9
201404	1827	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I will say that I don’t think we should pay too much attention to the interest expenses that we are going to incur with these different programs.	92	9
201404	1828	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	That’s just not something that we normally think about when making tough monetary policy decisions, and I don’t think we should start doing that now.	92	9
201404	1829	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	I am sensing from other commentary so far that option 3 seems to be at the intersection of many participant views expressed already, and that could be fine with me.	92	9
201404	1830	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	Although I have to say very seriously that I’m not sure I could pick option 3 out of a police lineup.	92	9
201404	1831	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	[Laughter].	92	9
201404	1832	2014-04-30	meeting	EVANS	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201404	1833	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you, President Evans.	92	9
201404	1834	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	President Pianalto.	92	9
201404	1835	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201404	1836	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	I was hoping that my last FOMC meeting would be fairly straightforward and would be on the shorter side.	92	9
201404	1837	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	But once I started to receive the staff memos and the agenda, I realized that wasn’t going to be the case.	92	9
201404	1838	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	Indeed, this may end up being one of the longest meetings I will have attended.	92	9
201404	1839	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	But, on a positive note, I have benefited from the learning gained by reading the excellent memos that were prepared by the staff and listening to today’s rich discussion, and the longer meeting gives me the opportunity to spend more quality time with my FOMC colleagues.	92	9
201404	1840	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	[Laughter]	92	9
201404	1841	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	On a more serious note, the number and length of the excellent background memos circulated to the Committee and today’s discussion make it clear that the issues surrounding the normalization of monetary policy are among the most complex the Committee has addressed in my 11 years of service.	92	9
201404	1842	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	As an aside, only Chair Yellen, President Lacker, and I were here the last time the FOMC began policy normalization, back in June of 2004 when we raised the fed funds rate, and it was a lot simpler back then.	92	9
201404	1843	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	But at the time, it didn’t seem very simple.	92	9
201404	1844	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	I know that today’s discussion is just a first step in a sequence of discussions that will be needed to eventually decide on a framework and to implement it.	92	9
201404	1845	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	And because these discussions and decisions will rest with all of you, and with my successor, Loretta Mester, who will be sitting at this seat at the next meeting, I am going to be brief in my comments today.	92	9
201404	1846	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	Rather than trying to give direct answers to the detailed questions put to the Committee, I will instead offer a few high-level comments that I hope will be of help to you when the time comes for the Committee to make some decisions.	92	9
201404	1847	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	There are two principles that I find to be helpful in evaluating the options for policy normalization.	92	9
201404	1848	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	First, I believe that in the long run the policy instrument should be the federal funds rate, or an augmented federal funds rate or a similar market rate should the federal funds rate not prove to be viable.	92	9
201404	1849	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	In my view, I think that the policy instrument should be one that is set by this Committee rather than just by the Board of Governors.	92	9
201404	1850	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	We have discussed the related governance issues before, and I continue to believe that they are important.	92	9
201404	1851	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	Basing policy on a rate like the federal funds rate clearly puts the responsibility where it is supposed to be, and that is with the FOMC.	92	9
201404	1852	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	And such a framework helps with public accountability and transparency.	92	9
201404	1853	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	Now, this doesn’t mean that the longer-run framework cannot use rates that are administered by the Board of Governors to support a target for the Committee’s policy instrument.	92	9
201404	1854	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	The second principle that I find helpful is that the best course of action will be one that does preserve a high degree of flexibility, as others have commented today.	92	9
201404	1855	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	As the staff memos point out, there are many uncertainties surrounding normalization.	92	9
201404	1856	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	As one example, we don’t know if the market behavior we observe in the small-scale testing of some of our tools will change in larger-scale implementation.	92	9
201404	1857	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	Broadly stated, the Federal Reserve has never been in these circumstances before, and preserving the ability to adjust tools and approaches is likely to prove important.	92	9
201404	1858	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	So using these two principles, I have just a few comments to make on normalization.	92	9
201404	1859	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	For one, during policy normalization, I believe that the Committee should continue to target the fed funds rate or a similar market rate.	92	9
201404	1860	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	If that rate is going to have a role in the longer-run operating framework, I think that it should have a role in the normalization period.	92	9
201404	1861	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	As the background memos describe, normalization approaches that make little or no use of the federal funds rate could kill off trading in the federal funds market, which would likely make it difficult to revive the market in the future.	92	9
201404	1862	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	My second comment is that it is desirable to draw on a range of tools during normalization to support the target for the federal funds rate, including overnight RRPs, the IOER rate, and the reserve draining tools.	92	9
201404	1863	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	The memos make it clear that each tool has limitations, but a combination of these tools should be useful when the time comes.	92	9
201404	1864	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	And finally, I would recommend proceeding with caution.	92	9
201404	1865	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	Over time, the staff has been able to develop a range of tools, and careful, gradual testing has taught us much about the efficacy of the tools.	92	9
201404	1866	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	The Federal Reserve will need to continue with cautious testing of both reverse repos and the Term Deposit Facility, while at the same time clearly communicating that testing does not bear on the likely timing of policy tightening.	92	9
201404	1867	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201404	1868	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201404	1869	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	President George.	92	9
201404	1870	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201404	1871	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	I, too, want to thank the staff for setting up today’s discussion.	92	9
201404	1872	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	I found it very helpful this morning as we did the Q&A.	92	9
201404	1873	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	And I, too, am going to answer the seven questions in summary fashion and be brief, given the discussion we have had already.	92	9
201404	1874	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	From a practical standpoint, the elevated size of our balance sheet is likely going to preclude targeting the fed funds rate in the early stages of policy normalization, so I agree that we should be prepared to lean on the overnight RRP during this phase of the process.	92	9
201404	1875	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	That said, at this stage, I would prefer not to abandon the federal funds target completely in a long-run strategy for steering short-term rates, because my own preference is for gradually returning the balance sheet to a more normal level.	92	9
201404	1876	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	At this point, however, I am open to enhanced testing across a range of tools to provide optionality with respect to our framework.	92	9
201404	1877	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	I would also be interested in testing strategies for facilities that could support highly competitive money markets by encouraging a broad range of depositories, including smaller banks, to transact in short-term money markets along the lines of the segregated balance accounts.	92	9
201404	1878	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	Testing a number of these tools may also provide some insight into how they interact with one another and affect short-term money markets.	92	9
201404	1879	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	My preferred approach right now is to eliminate, as others have noted, the extremes presented in the memos, as we will likely need to do some learning as we undertake normalization.	92	9
201404	1880	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	So, like others, I would focus primarily on options 2 and 3 at this stage.	92	9
201404	1881	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	In terms of the spread on the overnight RRPs during the normalization process, I would prefer to take an approach that maintains a wide spread to the IOER rate and move cautiously toward full allotment, and continue to utilize counterparty caps for the time being.	92	9
201404	1882	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	I would support taking steps toward moving the overnight RRP toward 15 basis points by the end of the year, simply to ensure we have a reasonably well-developed sense of how well it acts as a floor.	92	9
201404	1883	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	And after liftoff, I could also see even a wider spread between the overnight RRP rate and the IOER rate, perhaps as much as 25 basis points, to assess how well this rate acts as a floor.	92	9
201404	1884	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	If it’s effective, then we can possibly continue targeting the federal funds rate via a floor system.	92	9
201404	1885	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	If, however, it fails to act as a floor because of idiosyncratic reasons within the funds market, then we may want to consider expanding the use of term tools or reassess the use of the federal funds rate for implementing monetary policy.	92	9
201404	1886	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201404	1887	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	President Kocherlakota.	92	9
201404	1888	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201404	1889	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Like others, I want to thank the staff for their hard work and thoughtful analysis on the various memos.	92	9
201404	1890	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I thought this morning’s discussion was very enlightening for me, and I’ll echo President Fisher: I think that I, at least, would benefit from having further, possibly intermeeting, conversations about these issues as we move forward to a decision point, I guess in September.	92	9
201404	1891	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Before I launch into my prepared remarks, I just want to make one comment that there has been a lot of talk about learning by doing and learning as we go along.	92	9
201404	1892	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And I think this is, of course, going to be really important whatever we end up doing in terms of exit.	92	9
201404	1893	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But I do think we have to have a framework for thinking about exit that is robust enough to deal with the possibility that inflation risks might arise that would require us to raise rates relatively rapidly.	92	9
201404	1894	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And whatever we end up doing, whatever framework we set up should be robust enough to deal with that possibility.	92	9
201404	1895	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m not saying that we are there right now, but I think the idea of just groping along might not be possible given the macroeconomic objectives that we are trying to achieve.	92	9
201404	1896	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I’m going to begin by making three high-level comments about the deliberations and communications.	92	9
201404	1897	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	These first three high-level comments are relatively firm, and then I’m going to be a little more speculative and tentative in suggesting a way forward.	92	9
201404	1898	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I haven’t said this for a while, but it’s a pleasure to be able to say it.	92	9
201404	1899	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	A lot of my comments are going to agree with much that President Lacker had to say.	92	9
201404	1900	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s a pleasure to be able to say that after a long time.	92	9
201404	1901	2014-04-30	meeting		[Laughter]	92	9
201404	1902	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In terms of my high-level comments, the first comment relates to the 2011 exit-strategy principles.	92	9
201404	1903	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	In June 2011, the FOMC enunciated a set of exit-strategy principles with the support of all but one FOMC participant.	92	9
201404	1904	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Those principles emphasize the role of the federal funds rate target during exit, the likely role of asset sales during exit, the FOMC’s desire for a small long- run balance sheet, and the FOMC’s desire for an all-Treasury long-run balance sheet.	92	9
201404	1905	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I do think that when we communicate about our desired operational framework and, really, as we talk about it, we should be more connected back to those June 2011 principles.	92	9
201404	1906	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think our new communications about exit are less likely to be viewed as credible if we are not able to provide a convincing or compelling rationalization for why we are deviating from the June 2011 principles.	92	9
201404	1907	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That is my first comment.	92	9
201404	1908	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My second high-level comment is about communication, more generally, and monetary policy is fundamentally about expectations management.	92	9
201404	1909	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think that the FOMC needs to be able to communicate effectively about the likely evolution of its policy stance.	92	9
201404	1910	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think effective communication should be viewed as an important criterion for our choice of operational framework.	92	9
201404	1911	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I don’t think we should be thinking about this as let’s get the operational framework that will do the best job of reducing, say, the volatility of money market interest rates.	92	9
201404	1912	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think, really, we should be thinking about what is going to be the best framework that will allow us to be able to communicate effectively about the future arc of policy.	92	9
201404	1913	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	A complicated framework with multiple administrative rates and targets may well hinder that effective communication.	92	9
201404	1914	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And my final high-level comment is about the possible risk to financial stability.	92	9
201404	1915	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I mentioned in some of my questions this morning, I found the memos to be especially useful in my thinking and highlighting how an overnight RRP facility could increase the probability and severity of a financial panic.	92	9
201404	1916	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It is true that there is some upside with this facility as well, potentially mitigating these risks.	92	9
201404	1917	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But I see it as very asymmetrical.	92	9
201404	1918	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If the Federal Reserve’s creation of a new overnight facility was seen as being responsible for fostering a financial panic in any way, I think that, first of all, that would be bad from a policymaking point of view, but it would also, of course, be bad from a reputational point of view.	92	9
201404	1919	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Before we were to go forward with implementing such a facility, I would like to have in place very strong safeguards to mitigate adverse side effects of financial instability.	92	9
201404	1920	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I hinted at this morning in my questions, I am not convinced, at this stage at least, that the caps alone represent such a strong safeguard.	92	9
201404	1921	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Those are my three high-level, relatively firm comments.	92	9
201404	1922	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think we should be tying our discussions and communications about exit back to what we said in June 2011.	92	9
201404	1923	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We should be ensuring that our new operational framework is carefully designed to facilitate effective communication about the evolution of policy.	92	9
201404	1924	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And we should not adopt an overnight facility unless we have a great deal of confidence that we are not fostering the risk of financial instability.	92	9
201404	1925	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now I am going to be more tentative and turn to a suggested approach to exit and, as I said earlier, it is going to be similar to what President Lacker had to say.	92	9
201404	1926	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s a relatively simple version of option 5:  The FOMC specifies our interest rate target; the Board sets the interest rate on excess reserves equal to that target, and the Desk makes transactions in the federal funds market only to ensure the federal funds rate does not exceed the target set by the FOMC; and we don’t use draining of any kind to ensure that the federal funds rate does not fall below the target.	92	9
201404	1927	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s the approach, and I’ll state it again in a second.	92	9
201404	1928	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But why is this appropriate?	92	9
201404	1929	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	My starting point is that the FOMC achieves its goals of maximum employment and price stability by influencing the incentives of banks to make loans.	92	9
201404	1930	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Operationally, before 2008, the FOMC shaped banks’ incentives to make loans by varying a specified target for the federal funds rate.	92	9
201404	1931	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The crux of my argument is going to be that, given that we can pay IOER, the operational equivalent is now for the FOMC to specify a target for the maximum of the federal funds rate and interest on excess reserves.	92	9
201404	1932	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, we are always about shaping banks’ incentives, and there are always gaps that emerged.	92	9
201404	1933	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	If you look at the data, and President Lacker did a good job of talking about this, there are always gaps that emerged between our target rate and what was going on in money markets.	92	9
201404	1934	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Those gaps moved around, but it didn’t get in the way of our ability to influence aggregate demand, which is ultimately what we’re about.	92	9
201404	1935	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Why is it the right thing to be doing, the operational equivalent now, to specify a target for the maximum of the federal funds rate and the interest of excess reserves?	92	9
201404	1936	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Well, right now, at any point in time, banks have a choice among selling federal funds to other banks, holding reserves, and making loans.	92	9
201404	1937	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That means that the bank will only make a loan if its return is higher than both the fed funds rate and the interest on excess reserves.	92	9
201404	1938	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Before 2008, the interest on excess reserves was zero, so you didn’t have to worry about it.	92	9
201404	1939	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The only relevant alternative for the bank was the federal funds rate, and that’s why you could shape banks’ incentive to make loans by changing the target for the federal funds rate.	92	9
201404	1940	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	After 2008, the Federal Reserve was allowed to set the interest on excess reserves to be above zero.	92	9
201404	1941	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, there are two rates that can be relevant:  the federal funds rate or the interest on excess reserves.	92	9
201404	1942	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The key point is when the interest on excess reserves is higher than the federal funds rate, as it is now, banks then decide whether to make a loan by comparing the loan’s return with the IOER rate.	92	9
201404	1943	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The federal funds rate is irrelevant because it’s dominated by the interest rate on reserves.	92	9
201404	1944	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Now, I want to be clear about one thing here.	92	9
201404	1945	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	We do see banks borrowing at rates that are lower than IOER, but that’s their average borrowing cost.	92	9
201404	1946	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	On the margin, their financing costs are going to be equal to the IOER rate, because otherwise they would have an incentive to make that marginal increase in their borrowing to invest in the high IOER.	92	9
201404	1947	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	On the margin, they have to be indifferent between how they’re financing and IOER.	92	9
201404	1948	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There might be other borrowers that are able to get that low money market rate, but the banks themselves are marginally indifferent.	92	9
201404	1949	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	That’s what I see as a very simple exit strategy for the FOMC.	92	9
201404	1950	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	At each meeting, the Committee should specify our desired target interest rate.	92	9
201404	1951	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The Board of Governors should set the IOER rate equal to that target interest rate, and then the Desk should conduct operations in the fed funds market, if necessary, to ensure that the fed funds rate does not exceed that target interest rate.	92	9
201404	1952	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There is no draining in this approach.	92	9
201404	1953	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Basically, when the federal funds rate is lower than the IOER rate, it is just not influencing banks’ decisionmaking, so we can ignore it.	92	9
201404	1954	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	There are going to be gaps that are going to emerge between various short-term interest rates under this approach to monetary policy.	92	9
201404	1955	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Right now, the low federal funds rate means that the Federal Home Loan Banks are earning a very low overnight return on their investments relative to the IOER rate.	92	9
201404	1956	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	More generally, not all loans in the economy come from banks.	92	9
201404	1957	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Borrowers who have access to the commercial paper market may well be able to obtain loans at more favorable terms than those offered by banks.	92	9
201404	1958	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	But, as President Lacker pointed out, arbitrage considerations will limit such gaps.	92	9
201404	1959	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I will add that we do not have a theory to indicate to us whether such gaps are efficient or inefficient.	92	9
201404	1960	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And, finally, whether we did or not, that’s not the job of monetary policy.	92	9
201404	1961	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It is not to eliminate all gaps between short-term interest rates.	92	9
201404	1962	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The job of monetary policy is to shape aggregate demand by influencing the overall tightness of credit conditions, and we can, I believe, do that in an effective fashion by influencing banks’ incentives to make loans.	92	9
201404	1963	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I am proposing a change to the June 2011 exit principles, which emphasize the role of the federal funds rate target.	92	9
201404	1964	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	I think we can rationalize reorienting to the IOER rate by pointing out, as I have, that the federal funds rate is essentially irrelevant for bank decisionmaking when it is as low as it currently is.	92	9
201404	1965	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	And so the relevant margin is the IOER rate.	92	9
201404	1966	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201404	1967	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	92	9
201404	1968	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Tarullo.	92	9
201404	1969	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201404	1970	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	A number of people have characterized their remarks as tentative, and I will go a step further and say that mine are very tentative, particularly with respect to the final shape of an intermediate strategy.	92	9
201404	1971	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I’ll begin with a process point in which I actually have considerably greater confidence, and in some ways this picks up on a couple of President Kocherlakota’s introductory remarks.	92	9
201404	1972	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I guess because of the desire that most people have to maintain optionality, on the one hand, and, on the other hand, the fact that we all acknowledge there’s incomplete knowledge of a lot of what might happen as we begin to exit, I would suggest that for the next iteration of this exercise, as the staff generates materials for us, they try to generate them in a pretty concrete fashion that gives us a timeline in the following respects.	92	9
201404	1973	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	One, what we would expect the Chair to say in September or shortly thereafter, which is both a matter of communication, as President Kocherlakota was suggesting, and an indication of what we have to decide by then.	92	9
201404	1974	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Two, an understanding of what kinds of decisions we would subsequently need to make before we’re in a position to raise whatever the target interest rate would be at that time.	92	9
201404	1975	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	The reason I suggest doing it in that concrete way is that it will both let us see how the optionality and incomplete knowledge issues play out over time and test whether we feel comfortable going forward, or do we need to think this through more first.	92	9
201404	1976	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	It might also allow us to agree that we can put off some decisions that are interesting and otherwise might divert us along the way.	92	9
201404	1977	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Now, having just made that suggestion, I will—mindful of what Richard suggested earlier, which I think is absolutely right that we should bear in mind where we each think we may want to go—make a couple of comments about that potential final destination, particularly as it affects these intermediate decisions.	92	9
201404	1978	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I guess I would note first what some of you have alluded to, which is that the Federal Reserve has shifted its monetary policy regime a number of times over the course of its history—largely, presumably, in response to the changing circumstances of the financial system and the economy.	92	9
201404	1979	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	One would think that a particularly appropriate time for doing so again would be in reaction to a major financial crisis and a huge recession that followed, that were themselves connected, I think, to shortcomings in the combination of monetary and regulatory policies that existed in the period preceding the crisis.	92	9
201404	1980	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I mean, it does seem to me that the public policy framework had never taken account, ultimately, of the increasing integration of capital markets with conventional lending that occurred in the roughly 30 years preceding the crisis.	92	9
201404	1981	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	And, again, echoing something President Fisher said earlier, the decreased amount of intermediation by banks meant both that there were questions about the effectiveness of monetary policy transmission, but, quite apart from that, that it was possible to build up substantial risks to financial stability in a fashion that was outside the embrace either of monetary policy as then pursued or regulatory supervisory policy as then pursued.	92	9
201404	1982	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	As we sit here today, we’ve changed some of those circumstances, but not all.	92	9
201404	1983	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Unless one has enormous confidence in the ability and authority to develop time-varying macroprudential instruments that could fully compensate for a lot of the shortcomings of the pre- crisis period, I think we do want to consider, at least intellectually, the possibility of a shift to a monetary policy regime that takes account of that increasing integration of capital markets and traditional lending and that gives us another option in connection with financial stability.	92	9
201404	1984	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I’m thinking of the fact that as we have talked about financial stability from time to time over the last year or so, a number of people have made the very salient comment that they don’t want to be put into a situation of tightening when there’s such a macroeconomic problem, and so they’ve sort of raised the threshold for the amount of financial-stability risk that they want to see before doing so.	92	9
201404	1985	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	It seems to me that if we can develop a monetary policy regime that allows for a more nuanced reaction to financial-stability concerns without a general tightening of interest rates that we will have progressed substantially beyond where we were a decade ago when, as President Pianalto mentioned, the FOMC was engaged in its last exit.	92	9
201404	1986	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	That leads me to the overnight reverse repo facility.	92	9
201404	1987	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I have to say it seems to me that it’s highly desirable to have that tool and to continue to develop it and use it in the normalization process.	92	9
201404	1988	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I guess one could do without it, but it seems to me kind of clunky to try to proceed very far without using it.	92	9
201404	1989	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	But I do think that it has some potential—and this was most obvious in the memo that Governor Stein helped the staff with, or the staff helped Governor Stein with, one way or another [laughter], a collective process—and there are a lot of questions around the net impact on financial stability of the particular approach to the use of the facility and the idea of creating a huge amount of safe assets.	92	9
201404	1990	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	But I do think those are the kinds of questions that we need to tackle, and we have to think about things like the effect of our actions on risk premiums, particularly term premiums, and whether there is some way of pursuing a monetary policy that can, in a calibrated fashion, affect, for example, term premiums without resulting in just increases in short-term rates.	92	9
201404	1991	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	In a moment I will—notwithstanding the tentativeness—try to give a few provisional preferences on instruments or ideas that are or are not worth pursuing.	92	9
201404	1992	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I did want to say something, though, in response to President Lacker’s comments on supervision and matched books.	92	9
201404	1993	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	It is the case that we’ve been pursuing a set of regulatory and supervisory initiatives designed to constrain the development and maintenance of very large matched books with private actors on all sides because of the potential for runs that then result in cutting off funding to the other side of your book.	92	9
201404	1994	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I am unaware of—unless the Federal Reserve Bank of Richmond is doing something they’re not telling us about—any supervisory decisions within the LISCC to discourage the use of arbitrage with the IOER rate.	92	9
201404	1995	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	And I will say I think the fact that the FBOs are doing it and the domestic banks are not is kind of a good real-world experiment because the FBOs don’t have either the leverage ratio or the FDIC fee.	92	9
201404	1996	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	The FBOs do have our supervisors, and particularly the Federal Reserve Bank of New York supervisors, crawling all over them on the liquidity problems and liquidity issues that have existed.	92	9
201404	1997	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I actually think the explanation that the memo suggested is probably more likely to be correct.	92	9
201404	1998	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	In concluding, as I said, I don’t have a good sense at all, even a structure of a sense, of what our plan should be, but—I think this is probably what Bill English is looking for—in the interest of being able to say that some things are less worth pursuing and others more, I’ll at least state a couple of preferences.	92	9
201404	1999	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I have already talked about the overnight reverse repo.	92	9
201404	2000	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Like many other people, I think we should try to fix the federal funds rate, and I’d echo what President Pianalto and others have said along the way.	92	9
201404	2001	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	In terms of options, it doesn’t feel to me as though either option 1 or option 5 are really viable, but I do want to be tentative on that.	92	9
201404	2002	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Like Vice Chairman Dudley and President Lockhart, I’ve been having trouble seeing the utility of the Term Deposit Facility.	92	9
201404	2003	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Again, I don’t want to say I’d rule anything out, but that’s one that I find to be a less productive path to follow.	92	9
201404	2004	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I think I have already exceeded the number of useful things I have to say now, Madam Chair.	92	9

201404	2009	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I won’t try to answer all the questions.	92	9
201404	2010	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I’ll just try to venture some general thoughts.	92	9
201404	2011	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I also kind of had the same reaction that President Fisher did, which is I know we don’t need to be trying to answer the long-run question now.	92	9
201404	2012	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Nevertheless, I found it useful to start by thinking about the long-run question.	92	9
201404	2013	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	To me, the long-run question boils down to this:  In the long run, what kind of money do we see ourselves being the provider of?	92	9
201404	2014	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I think there are basically two possible responses.	92	9
201404	2015	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	One is, we go back to the old world in which we saw our franchise as providing currency and reserves.	92	9
201404	2016	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	That’s it.	92	9
201404	2017	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Alternatively, there’s a world in which we see our job as providing not only currency and reserves, but also some kind of safe asset that’s conceptually like Treasury bills— namely, our RRPs.	92	9
201404	2018	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	These are two pretty different mission statements, and they involve potentially different balance sheet sizes, obviously quite a bit larger in the case in which you take on this other thing.	92	9
201404	2019	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Let me start by just trying to think about the pros and cons of these two different tasks for the Fed.	92	9
201404	2020	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I actually think there’s a good argument to be made—obviously provisional at this point—for broadening the way we see doing our job, and it’s quite close to what Dan articulated in terms of financial stability.	92	9
201404	2021	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Think about our efforts on the regulatory side with the leverage ratio, we thought about the idea of doing capital surcharges for short-term wholesale funding, and so forth.	92	9
201404	2022	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	We’re basically, in various ways, trying to lean against the private sector’s efforts to create very, very short-term money-like claims, which they do when they basically hold long- term assets and fund them with repos.	92	9
201404	2023	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	We sort of don’t like guys doing that.	92	9
201404	2024	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Well, you might want to step back in all of this regulatory effort and say, “Why in the world are they doing it?”	92	9
201404	2025	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Well, they’re doing it because it’s cheap.	92	9
201404	2026	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	It’s cheap to fund at the very, very short end of the yield curve.	92	9
201404	2027	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Why is it cheap?	92	9
201404	2028	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Because somehow the world has not supplied enough of the sort of assets that money funds like:  very, very safe short-term money-like assets.	92	9
201404	2029	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	So they trade at a premium in price or, let’s say, they have very, very low yields.	92	9
201404	2030	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	If we don’t do it, the private sector will.	92	9
201404	2031	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	We can fight that with regulation, but, as Governor Tarullo says, doing it all with regulation is a heavy lift.	92	9
201404	2032	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	An alternative is to try to crowd it out by providing more of that stuff ourselves, and there’s some empirical evidence that in fact when, for example, the supply of Treasury bills goes up, the specialness premium goes down—that is to say, the yields go up and the private sector backs away and does less of that maturity transformation stuff.	92	9
201404	2033	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	The big, big picture here would be if we provide more repo-like stuff to the world, that eliminates some of the scarcity.	92	9
201404	2034	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	We’re saying to the private sector, we’re going to do some of the maturity transformation and you’re going to do less.	92	9
201404	2035	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Now, is this a radical thing to do?	92	9
201404	2036	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Is this a radical way to think about what we’re doing?	92	9
201404	2037	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I sort of thought about it like you guys did, like both President Fisher and Governor Tarullo did.	92	9
201404	2038	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	The world is really changing, and it’s more of a capital markets world, and so what is money has changed in some fundamental sense, and that would be the theory of the case.	92	9
201404	2039	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	President Lacker pointed out—we had a conversation offline about this—that, back in the day, we didn’t provide currency.	92	9
201404	2040	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	The private banks provided bank notes, and at some point the decision was made that that wasn’t all that good for financial stability, so we took that job away from the private sector.	92	9
201404	2041	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	This, in some ways, would be a bit of a modern-day analogue to that.	92	9
201404	2042	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	That would be sort of the big pitch for doing this at some point.	92	9
201404	2043	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Obviously, there are a bunch of cons.	92	9
201404	2044	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	It’s a serious expansion of what we do.	92	9
201404	2045	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	There are political issues.	92	9
201404	2046	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	There are all of the issues that we had earlier in the discussion about expanding the role of money funds, which I think are serious.	92	9
201404	2047	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I do think this idea of restricting our participation to purely Treasury-only or repo-only money funds changes the calculus somewhat.	92	9
201404	2048	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	While I would be averse to expanding our footprint in the conventional money fund sector, I might be less averse to doing it this way.	92	9
201404	2049	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	By the way, I’ve always been kind of fond of the segregated balance accounts, and actually doing this thing with the riskless money funds is just another, and maybe a more elegant, legally and kind of operationally, way to do it.	92	9
201404	2050	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I mean, think about it from the perspective of a corporate cash manager.	92	9
201404	2051	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	We’re just trying to find something that corporate cash managers who are dying to have a safe asset can put their money into without having a subsidy.	92	9
201404	2052	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	If they put it into Citibank, we worry that there’s some kind of a subsidy there.	92	9
201404	2053	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	If they put it into a money fund that’s commingling risky and riskless stuff, we worry there’s a subsidy there.	92	9
201404	2054	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	But if they put it into something that’s really riskless, maybe that’s actually not a bad outcome.	92	9
201404	2055	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	There’s the financial-stability flip side.	92	9
201404	2056	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Ex ante, it’s good to take maturity transformation away.	92	9
201404	2057	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Ex post, if you are really the elastic provider at a fixed rate, there’s this run risk, which seems like a very serious thing.	92	9
201404	2058	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	But I think that seems tractable.	92	9
201404	2059	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I mean, you basically cap it.	92	9
201404	2060	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	There are many ways to cap it, but then it’s back to just being like a world with more Treasury bills.	92	9
201404	2061	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	At any point in time if there’s a run, the price goes up, the yield goes down, and you have the same mechanism that you have normally.	92	9
201404	2062	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Anyway, this is all long-run stuff.	92	9
201404	2063	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Based on what I know now, which is pretty limited, I would be sympathetic to thinking about this.	92	9
201404	2064	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	There’s no reason to try to do anything that particularly marches in this direction right now.	92	9

201404	2067	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I don’t think that optionality goes away as long as the balance sheet is big, because as long as the balance sheet is as big as it is, we’re very likely to be doing some repo funding and learning about how that works, learning about how our counterparties behave and all of that.	92	9

201404	2085	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Then some poor bank did too much of that, and they’re scrambling for reserves at the end of the day.	92	9
201404	2086	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	You manage to print the high funds rate, but fine, we’ve got the funds rate up, but it’s now a rate that’s pretty divorced from other market rates.	92	9

201404	2113	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	[Laughter]	92	9
201404	2114	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	So, a great set of memos, very interesting discussion, and thanks for that.	92	9
201404	2115	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	My answers to the questions are buried in my remarks, and I’m going to start with, really, the practical short-term alternatives, and then come back a little bit to the more interesting longer-term issues in the end.	92	9
201404	2116	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	The practical need is to announce after the September meeting a plan for the mechanics of liftoff and for the foreseeable future, during which the balance sheet will be very large.	92	9
201404	2117	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	And for that period, there are two approaches that seem to me to be pretty workable.	92	9
201404	2118	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	First, I do find some attraction in a floor system in which the Committee uses the IOER rate and RRP rate to set a rough floor for short-term rates and doesn’t set a specific policy rate.	92	9
201404	2119	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	That is our option number 2.	92	9
201404	2120	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	A floor system would give the Committee the best control over short-term rates at the lowest cost.	92	9
201404	2121	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	We are going to be in a de facto floor system anyway for a while, due to the high level of reserves.	92	9
201404	2122	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	What this approach does is it just kind of accepts that reality and doesn’t complicate matters by adding a target rate.	92	9
201404	2123	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	For now, I would want to size that RRP facility at the lowest level that would achieve a reasonably tight floor and enable a clean liftoff.	92	9
201404	2124	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Part of that, I have to admit, is the cognitive dissonance that Charlie Plosser mentioned—simultaneously noting that money market funds are a grave threat to financial stability as well as a principal conduit for our monetary policy.	92	9
201404	2125	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Now, the idea of limiting our contacts to government money funds does have the attraction of addressing that problem.	92	9
201404	2126	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	How large would that be?	92	9
201404	2127	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I’m not sure, but for me, at the beginning, it would be in the hundreds of billions, not the trillions.	92	9
201404	2128	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	And what I would do is gradually move the RRP up closer to the IOER rate to achieve the desired size.	92	9
201404	2129	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	What would that spread be?	92	9
201404	2130	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I don’t know.	92	9
201404	2131	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	It would be narrower than the current 20 basis points.	92	9
201404	2132	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I am not inclined to move to a full-allotment RRP, which would be a promise to supply a new risk-free asset to the world.	92	9
201404	2133	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	There is little doubt in my mind that the world would take us up on that in a crisis.	92	9
201404	2134	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I would, as we discussed earlier, experiment with size caps and with using the price mechanism—for example, by switching to a single-price auction if demand exceeds the quantity limit.	92	9
201404	2135	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	These would be appropriate subjects for testing.	92	9
201404	2136	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I see two main objections to this explicit floor system during normalization.	92	9
201404	2137	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	The first is that the federal funds market, already highly idiosyncratic, would lose further liquidity under a floor system and maybe cease to be a useful benchmark.	92	9
201404	2138	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	There would be problems of contract frustration, and it might be difficult down the road, it is said, to return to a corridor system based on the federal funds rate.	92	9
201404	2139	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	It seems to me that we could meet the first set of issues by broadening the federal funds rate to include Eurodollar rates, maybe other unsecured interbank borrowing rates.	92	9
201404	2140	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	It also seems to me that if a future Committee wanted to return in the longer term to a market-based target, like the federal funds rate or the broader overnight bank funding rate, or, for that matter, to a secured rate like GC repo, I believe it would be straightforward to do so.	92	9
201404	2141	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	So I’m not too worried about preserving options down the road.	92	9
201404	2142	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I think that they are going to be there for us if we want to use them.	92	9
201404	2143	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Actually, the more difficult objection for me is that the size of the balance sheet is a matter of choice under a floor system.	92	9
201404	2144	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	It’s not that I’m particularly in a rush to shrink the balance sheet now, but I’m just not yet comfortable with the idea today that we should permanently and massively enlarge the balance sheet.	92	9
201404	2145	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Fortunately, I don’t think we’re making that decision now.	92	9
201404	2146	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I don’t see the use of a floor system during this interim period as implying a longer-term choice to do so.	92	9
201404	2147	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I would be comfortable with this explicit floor approach during normalization.	92	9
201404	2148	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Second, during liftoff and normalization, I could also get comfortable with a corridor system in which RRP sets the floor, the IOER rate sets the ceiling, and the Committee targets something in between, either the federal funds or overnight bank funding rate.	92	9
201404	2149	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	There would be perhaps less-precise control, which I don’t see at all as a fatal flaw.	92	9
201404	2150	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	This would be a natural extension of the current practice, as Governor Stein just noted, and more familiar to the markets.	92	9
201404	2151	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	It probably represents the easiest, least risky, least surprising thing to do, and that actually recommends it quite a lot to me.	92	9
201404	2152	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	If this system is to work, there would need to be a fix to the federal funds rate, and there would be some mechanics to work out, but there is just about enough time to do that.	92	9
201404	2153	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I have to admit, this approach seems slightly less elegant to me and less well-linked to the evolution of the credit markets.	92	9
201404	2154	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Intermediation is moving outside of the banking system, and it is moving to secured rates.	92	9
201404	2155	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	There is a feeling that we would be going back in time to a comfortable old system rather than allowing our policy framework to evolve to suit the markets of the future.	92	9
201404	2156	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	The second approach is also more expensive.	92	9
201404	2157	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	If the spread remains about 15 basis points on $3 trillion in reserves, that is $4½ billion per year in unnecessary cash payments, mainly to large foreign banks that hold one-half of the reserves and growing.	92	9
201404	2158	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	So I am concerned about the remittances, and I would really want to see the best estimates we can come up with to talk about this.	92	9
201404	2159	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	It could be significant, and it could actually make it more likely at the margin that we have a period of zero remittances, which would not be a positive thing.	92	9
201404	2160	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	But I think either of those two approaches could work pretty well.	92	9
201404	2161	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I would leave the other tools on the table without emphasizing them.	92	9
201404	2162	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	The idea of testing term deposit facilities seems okay.	92	9
201404	2163	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I also don’t look at the draining tools, as such, as particularly useful or important.	92	9
201404	2164	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I see them as expensive and principally cosmetic at this point, when we have $3 trillion in reserves.	92	9
201404	2165	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I wouldn’t have any urge to use them.	92	9
201404	2166	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	I guess I would use them if I had to, which would take me into option 4, if the repo floor turns out to be too leaky.	92	9
201404	2167	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Finally, let me turn to the longer-term issues.	92	9
201404	2168	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	And, again, these are not things we need to decide today, but they’re on the list of questions, so I’ll go ahead and talk about them.	92	9
201404	2169	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	And I would include in this group such questions as, what is the ideal system?	92	9
201404	2170	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Floor system, corridor system?	92	9
201404	2171	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Should we target a large balance sheet or a small one?	92	9
201404	2172	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	What kind of rate?	92	9
201404	2173	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	How will we handle the governance questions around IOER at the end of the day?	92	9
201404	2174	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	And, finally, should we be using the policy framework to do things other than implement traditional monetary policy in a narrow sense, specifically to improve financial stability?	92	9
201404	2175	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	On the first, I do think that over the longer term, in our financial system, an administered floor approach may be simpler and cheaper than a corridor with a market rate.	92	9
201404	2176	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	At the same time, I kind of think that a large balance sheet might prove to be a magnet for trouble over time, and those two considerations pull me in opposite directions, I admit.	92	9
201404	2177	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	So I tentatively land on a floor system with the smallest possible balance sheet.	92	9
201404	2178	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	But that brings you to the really interesting question, I think, that Governor Tarullo and Governor Stein were all over, and that is the use of the balance sheet for financial-stability purposes.	92	9
201404	2179	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Very, very interesting questions, and I don’t have a lot to add on them here today, but I think those are the things we are going to be talking about for years to come.	92	9
201404	2180	2014-04-30	meeting	POWELL	Thank you, Madam Chair.	92	9
201404	2181	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Well, thank you, Governor Powell, and thank you to everyone.	92	9
201404	2182	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	I think we’ve had a great discussion of this.	92	9
201404	2183	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	We have really dug into the issues.	92	9
201404	2184	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	I’m not going to try to provide a summary, but, listening to the go-round, I will work with the staff to try to see if we have found some common ground here.	92	9
201404	2185	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	While I know a wide range of views were expressed, I think I can begin to see where some common ground lies.	92	9
201404	2186	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	We will think about how best to structure the presentations and decisions at our next meeting in June, when we can hopefully move further in a practical direction of finding a way forward.	92	9
201404	2187	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	But I think for today we have had a great discussion.	92	9
201404	2188	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Let me also note at this point that the Board meeting, which was operating simultaneously with the FOMC meeting, now concludes.	92	9
