201404	3294	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	You have high profit margins, high stock market valuation relative to book value, and healthy balance sheets.	13	1
201404	3295	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Yet, as we saw in the first quarter, business fixed investment declined.	13	1
201404	3296	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	If we’re going to get a meaningful pickup in economic growth this year, it seems to me that investment and housing have to be the main sources.	13	1
201404	3297	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	The conversations that we’ve had with smaller businesses do suggest greater optimism with respect to investment, but we’ll see if that actually materializes.	13	1
201404	3298	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	My views on inflation haven’t changed much, either.	13	1
201404	3299	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	On the margin, I am becoming more confident that the year-over-year inflation readings will drift up over the remainder of the year, and I think this might happen even faster than in the Tealbook forecast.	13	1
201404	3300	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Part of this reflects the fact that there were important base effects a year ago in terms of prices for imports excluding petroleum, which were unusually soft last spring and summer.	13	1
201404	3301	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Medical costs were held down by the cut in Medicare disbursements last April, so that will drop out of the year-over-year numbers shortly.	13	1
201404	3302	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But part of it reflects my view that some of the other trends that have held down inflation are reversing, and in that bucket I would put prices for medical goods and services, which were unusually weak for a while, and owners’ equivalent rent.	13	1
201404	3303	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	We’ve already seen a flattening out of the core inflation measures on a year-over-year basis.	13	1
201404	3304	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	So I expect that the bottom is in and these readings will creep higher, perhaps more rapidly than in the Tealbook forecast.	13	1
201404	3305	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Finally, I have a few thoughts on the recent debate about what drives compensation trends—short-term unemployment versus long-term unemployment.	13	1
201404	3306	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I’ve read a bunch of the papers, and where I come out is described by five points.	13	1
201404	3307	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	First of all, one has to be very skeptical about the econometric results when you have two variables that are so collinear, and so it’s very sensitive to the time period you pick.	13	1
201404	3308	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	There’s a great deal of skepticism just because of the collinearity.	13	1
201404	3309	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Second, I think it does depend on why one turns out to be long-term unemployed.	13	1
201404	3310	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I think there are two possibilities.	13	1
201404	3311	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	One is, you’re long-term unemployed because you don’t have the appropriate job skills.	13	1
201404	3312	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	This is a case of mismatch.	13	1
201404	3313	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	That’s very different from the other reason why you might be long-term unemployed:  You might have lost your job during the deep recession, and there’s a lack of labor demand.	13	1
201404	3314	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	So you’re long-term unemployed not because you don’t have the right skills, but just because there’s a lack of labor demand for you.	13	1
201404	3315	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	In the first case, you’re not very attached to the labor market, but in the second case, you’re just unlucky.	13	1
201404	3316	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	You’re long-term unemployed due to bad timing.	13	1
201404	3317	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	In the current situation, I would expect that a greater proportion of those who are currently long-term unemployed are in the unlucky category as opposed to not having the appropriate skills.	13	1
201404	3318	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	A third observation is that the impact of the long-term unemployed on wages presumably depends on whether there’s an excess supply of short-term unemployed or not.	13	1
201404	3319	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	If there are plenty of short-term unemployed, I think they’ll get the opportunities first, because we see that there’s a clear bias against people who are long-term unemployed.	13	1
201404	3320	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	As long as there are plenty of short-term unemployed, this will show up as the long-term unemployed being relatively unattached.	13	1
201404	3321	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But once the pool of short-term unemployed is depleted, the long-term unemployed will become more relevant in terms of labor market supply.	13	1
201404	3322	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	So their impact on the labor market and wages will increase as the labor market tightens.	13	1
201404	3323	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I don’t think it’s quite as static as some of these reports suggest.	13	1
201404	3324	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Fourth, as is generally the case, I don’t think this is black or white.	13	1
201404	3325	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I can’t believe that, if you’re unemployed beyond 26 weeks, something happens at that point; I think it’s probably a gradient.	13	1
201404	3326	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	And I’m pretty comfortable with the idea that, on average, as you become more unemployed for a longer period of time, you become somewhat less attached to the labor market.	13	1
201404	3327	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	But the idea that you’re either attached or unattached seems way too simple.	13	1
201404	3328	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Fifth, it seems obvious to me that labor demand is weak, completely apart from this debate.	13	1
201404	3329	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Some people are acting as though this is the way to measure whether the labor market is weak.	13	1
201404	3330	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	And, if you look at the labor market in terms of the transition from unemployment to employment, that’s well below the historical average for all durations of unemployment.	13	1
201404	3331	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	So you could set this whole story aside, and you would still reach the conclusion that labor market demand is quite weak.	13	1
201404	3332	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I think this whole debate has assumed greater significance than, in fact, it probably deserves.	13	1
201404	3333	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201404	3334	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	13	1
201404	3335	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Governor Tarullo.	13	1
201404	3336	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1
201404	3337	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I subscribe to the view that many of you have already stated—that we can expect a bounceback in the economy this quarter—but I would also subscribe to the Evans-Dudley gloss that we need to be watching to see whether this actually gets us back to the track that people were expecting.	13	1
201404	3338	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	The only thing I would add to what many of you have said is that I think the major downside risks are now actually external, which, in one sense, is good news, because the US economy, we believe, does have some resilience and is going to have some self-sustaining momentum.	13	1
201404	3339	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	On the other hand, we can’t do much about the external risks.	13	1
201404	3340	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	The two that I’ll identify—and this is hardly a surprise—are China, on which views among even the well informed seem to differ considerably right now, and Ukraine–Russia.	13	1
201404	3341	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	The latter is obviously geopolitical in origin and just a wildcard that’s not susceptible to prediction by businesspeople or a particularly salient response by economic policymakers.	13	1
201404	3342	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Like many of you—most recently, Bill—I want to return to some of the labor market issues that we’ve been discussing.	13	1
201404	3343	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Actually, these comments, I think, will complement what Vice Chairman Dudley and President Kocherlakota have been saying.	13	1
201404	3344	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	As people will recall, I’ve been suggesting that we should be pretty cautious about drawing too strong a conclusion with respect to current labor market problems based on expectations or extrapolations from past trends.	13	1
201404	3345	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	We have a very different set of circumstances from even the serious recessions of the post–World War II period—most notably, in the persistence of high levels of long-term unemployment.	13	1
201404	3346	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Historically, of course, national rates of short-term unemployment and long- term unemployment pretty much move together, and that has not been the case here.	13	1
201404	3347	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	So I continue to be skeptical of arguments such as the one asserting that the detachment of the long- term unemployed from the labor market based on the observation that the Beveridge curve shifts seem to have been driven by long-term unemployment.	13	1
201404	3348	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	If you look at a long-term unemployment Beveridge curve from past recessions, it’s always shifted more than a short-term unemployment curve.	13	1
201404	3349	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	One difference is that in past postwar recessions, we had neither the long- term unemployed nor the persistence of high numbers of long-term unemployed.	13	1
201404	3350	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	So the looping back occurred fairly quickly.	13	1
201404	3351	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Similarly, it seems a little questionable to rely on the relationship between national unemployment and inflation rates as observed prior to the mid-1990s, when core inflation declined significantly and the Phillips curve appeared to flatten.	13	1
201404	3352	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	That’s why I’ve thought that we can’t really draw much from such observations that would give us a particularly good prediction about what’s going to be happening in current circumstances.	13	1
201404	3353	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	So I’ve suggested that we need to be practical in our responses.	13	1
201404	3354	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Now, since the last meeting, not much has changed in the labor market, for reasons many of you have already stated.	13	1
201404	3355	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	What has changed, I think—and we already heard some examples of it yesterday afternoon and this morning—is that we’re starting to get some empirical work specifically investigating the question of whether the long-term unemployed constitute an important element of labor market slack in the current, historically atypical circumstance of high levels for an extended period.	13	1
201404	3356	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	And I’d just add in passing that I think President Kocherlakota’s focus on the part-time employed for economic reasons probably complements this, because there’s been a pretty strong correlation between the long-term unemployed and the part-time employed for economic reasons over the past five years or so.	13	1
201404	3357	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	A number of researchers have begun to look at state or metropolitan-area data, thereby moving from a single data set to dozens of data sets with varying levels of unemployment and wage movement.	13	1
201404	3358	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	There are at least three examples of this work that I wanted to mention—two from the Board and one external.	13	1
201404	3359	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	First, Michael Kiley’s working paper “An Evaluation of the Inflationary Pressure Associated with Short- and Long-term Unemployment” examines the historical, cross-city, over-time relationships between CPI inflation and both short- and long- term unemployment rates.	13	1
201404	3360	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	And he found that both rates exerted equal pressure on prices.	13	1
201404	3361	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Second, Christopher Smith has been researching cross-state variations in wages and measures of labor slack and has found similar results for wage inflation—that is, that long-term unemployment and short-term unemployment historically exert the same kinds of influence on wages.	13	1
201404	3362	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Christopher’s early results are very robust to a range of wage measures and across various time periods.	13	1
201404	3363	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	This work is not yet in a working paper, because poor Christopher has been stuck in Tealbook hell.	13	1
201404	3364	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	But it will likely be available in working paper form over the next couple of weeks.	13	1
201404	3365	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Third, looking at current data, Peter Hooper and his colleagues in the research department at Deutsche Bank have found a negative relationship between long-term unemployment rates and wage growth in states in which short-term unemployment has declined to something close to normal levels.	13	1
201404	3366	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	This is consistent with the intuition that Bill was expressing a minute ago.	13	1
201404	3367	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	An interesting point about this research is that Hooper and his coauthors do not find such a relationship in states in which a short-term unemployment gap remains.	13	1
201404	3368	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	This contrast suggests, again, the intuition Bill just stated—that employers as a group will not move to hire the long- term unemployed in large numbers until the pool of short-term unemployed has become somewhat depleted.	13	1
201404	3369	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	The Hooper finding is also congruent with analysis by the Board’s staff showing that multiple measures of labor slack, including the share of the workforce employed part time for economic reasons and the share of marginally attached or discouraged workers, move toward their pre-recession levels as a state short-term unemployment rate normalizes.	13	1
201404	3370	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	I do want to note in passing that the Hooper finding depends on estimates of unemployment gaps, which are, as everyone in this room can attest, subject to debate.	13	1
201404	3371	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	So these lines of research are obviously not conclusive on the issue of how much labor market slack remains, and there’s nothing in them to contradict my intuition that there’s been some structural labor market damage.	13	1
201404	3372	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	But I do think they provide some empirical support for the position that the long-term unemployed continue to affect labor market conditions, and that there remains considerable labor market slack that could be reduced through higher levels of aggregate demand.	13	1
201404	3373	2014-04-30	meeting	TARULLO	Thank you, Madam Chair.	13	1

201404	3378	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	So, given that, I thought I would just mention a couple of data items that caught my attention recently.	13	1
201404	3379	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	One is the housing market, and a number of you have already spoken about the housing market.	13	1
201404	3380	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Here, the interesting set of facts to be reconciled is that, on the one hand, it seems fair to characterize the data on home sales—both new and existing as well as starts and permits—as being weaker than expected, so quantity measures look soft.	13	1

201404	3385	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	President Lockhart mentioned a few of the specific factors, and I think that feels reasonably compelling and worth thinking about.	13	1

201404	3387	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	So, what the quantity data may be telling us is that we’ve got a little bit of softness in prices yet to come.	13	1
201404	3388	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I think it will be interesting to keep an eye on that.	13	1
201404	3389	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	The other thing—and this is an oddball thing—is that I saw this article in the Wall Street Journal last week on the market for catastrophe bonds.	13	1
201404	3390	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	If you don’t know what catastrophe bonds are, basically, you give your money over, and if no bad disaster happens—no tornado, hurricane, or earthquake—then a year later or three years later, you get all of your money back with interest.	13	1
201404	3391	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	If there is one, then it’s like a default on your bonds—you lose your principal.	13	1
201404	3392	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	This market has seen a very big surge in issuance and a decline in yields, which was the Wall Street Journal story.	13	1
201404	3393	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I got interested in this.	13	1
201404	3394	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Now, why in the world would you care about this market?	13	1
201404	3395	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	It’s a small market; it’s surely not systemic in any sense of the word.	13	1
201404	3396	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	It matters only if you’re into nerdy identification stuff [laughter].	13	1
201404	3397	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Think about the inference problem we face when we look at corporate bond spreads narrowing.	13	1
201404	3398	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Corporate bond spreads have been coming down, and we’re always saying, “Well, it could be that risk premiums are going down, and returns to investors are going to be lower.”	13	1
201404	3399	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	But it could be a strengthening economy—right?—with lower defaults in the period ahead.	13	1
201404	3400	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	The staff does various things to try to tease these apart—for example, looking at short-term versus long- term credit spreads.	13	1
201404	3401	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	And there’s some notion that the short-term stuff is more default informative.	13	1
201404	3402	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	I think about the beauty of catastrophe bonds.	13	1
201404	3403	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	The spreads on those are going to narrow for one of two reasons—either risk appetite, broadly defined, or higher probability of a disaster.	13	1
201404	3404	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	But as long as acts of God are not pro-cyclical, they’re totally uninformative about the real economy—that you lose money when there’s a hurricane or something.	13	1
201404	3405	2014-04-30	meeting	STEIN	Here’s the idea:  If you saw catastrophe bond yields and junk bond yields moving very closely together, the only thing that these guys intersect on is the demand, the risk sentiment.	13	1

201404	3445	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	At our last meeting, I tried to summarize some of your comments in terms of how they answer a few key questions that bear on our policy decisions, and I thought that, with your indulgence, I would try to do the same thing again today.	13	1

201404	3450	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	A final policy-relevant question is, what factors could materially alter our forecast of a gradual return of employment and inflation to mandate-consistent levels, or, put differently, what are some of the notable risks to the outlook?	13	1
201404	3451	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	On the question of labor market improvement, I heard general agreement around the table that the labor market is continuing to improve, and that spending is most likely strong enough to see continued gradual improvement, although certainly a number of people—President Lockhart, Vice Chairman Dudley, President Evans, and Governor Tarullo—all suggested that the jury actually remains out at this point about just how large a pickup we’re likely to see in economic growth.	13	1
201404	3452	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	That said, almost all of you noted that there’s been very little shift in your forecasts, and I interpret them as generally in the same ballpark as the Tealbook’s.	13	1
201404	3453	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	The March employment report and incoming spending and production data—with the exception of housing, which a number of you commented on—are consistent with a diminished risk that the softer data we saw on employment and spending early this year signal any persistent loss of momentum.	13	1
201404	3454	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	A number of you—Presidents Fisher, Lockhart, and Plosser as well as others—commented on seeing firms somewhat more growth-oriented and on improved business optimism.	13	1
201404	3455	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Certainly, we are seeing payroll growth move up.	13	1
201404	3456	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	The three-month averages of total and private payroll gains are now around 180,000 a month, whereas at the time of the February report, they were running around 130,000.	13	1
201404	3457	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	And, as many of you noted, other metrics pertaining to the labor market—such as the workweek, labor force participation, and the employment-to- population ratio—also point to continued improvement.	13	1
201404	3458	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Now, a number of you commented on the issue of how much slack remains in the labor market, and that is an important issue that will bear on our decision of how long we should wait before beginning to tighten policy.	13	1
201404	3459	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	I took note of comments by Presidents Fisher and George, for example; you do hear reports of labor shortages in some sectors and occupations.	13	1
201404	3460	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	But, as President Evans and others noted, this is something of a chronic situation—there are areas in which we do persistently see shortages.	13	1
201404	3461	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	President Lacker described a very interesting piece of research at the Richmond Fed that, if I’m summarizing it properly, involved creating an index of underutilization of labor, with weights reflecting transition rates into employment.	13	1
201404	3462	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	And, as I understood the conclusion of this research, it is that, while in some sense there is more slack in the labor market than would be captured by U-3, the behavior of this broader measure of underutilization relative to U-3 is, nevertheless, very similar to that in past business cycles.	13	1
201404	3463	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Therefore, U-3 remains a good single indicator of the state of the labor market.	13	1
201404	3464	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	A number of you commented on a topic I was going to discuss, and I will now curtail my comments on this, which pertains to work by Krueger and others about long- and short-term unemployment and their effects on inflation.	13	1
201404	3465	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	I can’t hope to adequately summarize the arguments that Vice Chairman Dudley, Governor Tarullo, and others made about this, but I think the bottom line here is that we should be quite skeptical about jumping to the conclusion that it’s only short-term unemployment, which is now down to normal levels, that drives inflation.	13	1
201404	3466	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	There are a number of reasons for being skeptical about that conclusion.	13	1

201404	3471	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	A number of you—President Kocherlakota and Governor Tarullo—noted that part-time employment also seems very high in comparison with U-3, and that it appears to put pressure on wages in the cross-section work that President Kocherlakota described.	13	1
201404	3472	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	I thought President Rosengren also described interesting empirical work that suggests that a lot of individuals out of the labor market, including retirees, are likely to reenter the workforce as the expansion continues.	13	1
201404	3473	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	And I think the bottom line of that—I’m not positive—is that there is more slack than what would be captured in U-3.	13	1
201404	3474	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Certainly, if a portion of the decline in labor force participation reflects cyclical factors and that labor force participation is likely to increase, then we may see an outcome in which—and this is similar to what’s in the Tealbook—instead of declining further because of ongoing secular trends, labor force participation could just flatten out.	13	1

201404	3479	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	I think the story here is that nominal wage growth may be currently held down by the fact that while some firms during the downturn cut wages, many were either unwilling or unable to cut wages as much as they would have liked.	13	1
201404	3480	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	That may, on the one hand, explain why wage growth and inflation didn’t actually fall as much as we might have expected during the downturn, but now it explains why, as the labor market is improving, wage growth isn’t picking up as much as we might expect.	13	1
201404	3481	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	I believe President Kocherlakota mentioned some anecdotal information that’s consistent with that.	13	1

201404	3492	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	On the related question of just how much slack remains in labor markets, I would say that I have also been spending quite a bit of time thinking about the distinction between long- and short-term unemployment—the work by Krueger and others that Governor Tarullo and Vice Chairman Dudley discussed in detail—and have been reading with interest recent papers on this topic.	13	1
201404	3493	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	I agree with their conclusion that this work really casts doubt on the general conclusion that there is a big difference between short- and long-term unemployment and their effects on inflation.	13	1
201404	3494	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	I would note, though, that even if it were the case that long-term unemployment has a notably weaker influence on inflation than short-term unemployment, that doesn’t quite settle the question of how long-term unemployment should affect our policy decisions.	13	1
201404	3495	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	The evidence, as I read it, suggests that the long-term unemployed really look no different in terms of their characteristics than the short-term unemployed.	13	1
201404	3496	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	They’re individuals who want to work.	13	1
201404	3497	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	In that sense, they are quite employable, in spite of the fact that employers may have a tendency to pass them over at least until the firms are finding that it’s becoming much harder to find qualified applicants.	13	1
201404	3498	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Until they become reemployed, it could be the case that they do exert less pressure on wages and prices, although I am skeptical of that.	13	1
201404	3499	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	But, even if they do, it looks as though more rapid expansion of aggregate demand would certainly help the long-term unemployed get jobs, and the potential cost of that might be higher inflation.	13	1
201404	3500	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Well, at this point, inflation is running well below our 2 percent objective.	13	1
201404	3501	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	So, in pushing aggregate demand to try to bring them back into the labor force and into jobs, it seems to me that we currently face no tradeoff in terms of our dual mandate.	13	1
201404	3502	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	To me, the right policy response is to provide enough accommodation to push down long-term unemployment, along with other manifestations of underutilization, such as the still very low rate of labor force participation and the high share of part-time employment for economic reasons.	13	1
201404	3503	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	If we follow a strategy like that, a day may come when, ultimately, we will face a tradeoff between our objectives.	13	1
201404	3504	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	If that happens, that’s something we will have to address, I presume, using a balanced approach.	13	1
201404	3505	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	But we’re not at that point yet.	13	1

201404	3737	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	As currently envisioned, the Committee’s strategy is to end the open-ended QE program later in 2014 and, if all goes well, to begin raising the policy rate sometime in 2015.	23	2
201404	3738	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	Alternatives A and C are meant to address, at least in part, what the Committee’s options may be in the case that the economy performs either better than expected, alternative C, or worse than expected, alternative A.	23	2
201404	3739	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	The phrase “performs better or worse than expected” is a judgment made by the Committee.	23	2
201404	3740	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	As currently stated, the response to a better- than-expected economy is to increase the pace of the taper.	23	2
201404	3741	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	The response to a weaker-than- expected economy is to pause in the tapering process.	23	2
201404	3742	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	The response to a stronger-than-expected economy is to taper at a faster pace and presumably end the purchase program sooner.	23	2
201404	3743	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	Given our experience at the June meeting and the September meeting last year, I think that both of these possible policy actions would move expectations of future policy in the desired direction and would have a substantial effect on financial market conditions.	23	2
201404	3744	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	However, conceptually, we are viewing the taper as coming to an end in the second half of 2014—that is, the pace of purchases would hit zero and evidently remain there for a period of time.	23	2
201404	3745	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	The size of the balance sheet, given our reinvestment strategy, would remain fixed.	23	2
201404	3746	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	Because we are, in essence, approaching a fixed balance sheet, the policy value of choosing A or C is diminishing as we reduce the pace of purchases to smaller and smaller amounts.	23	2
201404	3747	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	This may be leaving the Committee without good responses to adverse developments in either direction during the remainder of 2014.	23	2

201404	3750	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	And alternative C could envision not just pushing the pace of purchases to zero more quickly, but also, in the event that the economy were actually performing quite robustly, having a so-called negative tapering, or a controlled reduction in the size of the balance sheet.	23	2
201404	3751	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	As options about how to respond to developments in either direction, these would be more effective and maybe should be the way we should think about this as we proceed through the rest of 2014 and before we get to the point in 2015 at which we’d actually be thinking about raising the policy rate.	23	2
201404	3752	2014-04-30	meeting	BULLARD	The bottom line of my comment is that alternatives A and C, should we need to employ them, may not have a large enough effect as we get to smaller and smaller asset purchase amounts.	23	2

201404	3786	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	It doesn’t appear, though, from the March SEP that many participants have yet lowered that lower-than-normal long-run funds rate.	23	2
201404	3787	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	So I think this is a topic the Committee should continue to discuss, because if we’re going to consider allowing r* to vary over time in the context of our policy rules, this is going to be very hard to communicate, and we’re going to have to be very clear about how we put a discipline around making those decisions.	23	2
201404	3788	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	What theory and what evidence are we going to use to justify that?	23	2
201404	3789	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	The kind of discussion in the Tealbook was a useful beginning to having that discussion.	23	2
201404	3790	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	But it’s a very complicated issue, and it’s a very difficult issue for the Committee to face.	23	2
201404	3791	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	I would not want us to do this or to become a case of the tail wagging the dog.	23	2
201404	3792	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	That is, we decide for some reason that we want lower interest rates, and then we take down our estimate of r*—that’s not the right way to think about this.	23	2
201404	3793	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	We need a more consistent, disciplined way of talking and thinking about this.	23	2
201404	3794	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	We don’t have that yet, and it’s likely to be problematic for us unless we begin to address it.	23	2
201404	3795	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	My own view is that the bar should be quite high for allowing that free parameter to move around, as I think it’ll make our communications quite difficult, because then we’d have to explain why and how and when we change it.	23	2
201404	3796	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	So the bar needs to be very high for moving that around.	23	2
201404	3797	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	Finally, I’ll reiterate what I said earlier and yesterday as well.	23	2
201404	3798	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	I think it would be prudent for us to begin thinking about our balance sheet more directly, particularly in the context of the exit principles that we’ve talked about before, integrating a discussion of how we’re going to go about shrinking the balance sheet in the context of the discussion that we had yesterday.	23	2
201404	3799	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	I think those two things are linked.	23	2
201404	3800	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	And how we integrate those into our discussion of reducing purchases and then perhaps beginning to reduce reinvestments—maybe that becomes a continuous path.	23	2
201404	3801	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	I don’t know.	23	2
201404	3802	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	But we need to begin thinking about that, so I encourage us to put that on the table for discussion soon.	23	2
201404	3803	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	But for today, I can support alternative B and the language.	23	2
201404	3804	2014-04-30	meeting	PLOSSER	Thank you.	23	2

201404	3826	2014-04-30	meeting	PIANALTO	Thank you.	23	2
201404	3827	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	I, too, support alternative B in terms of a further reduction in our asset purchases.	23	2
201404	3828	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	The economy continues to make meaningful cumulative gains, and inflation is widely expected to gradually move higher after having been held down by a number of temporary factors.	23	2
201404	3829	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	As the economy strengthens, though, I think there are a couple of communication issues worth considering, and both of these happen to be along the lines that President Plosser has already raised—one related to our longer-term forward guidance and the other the reinvestment policy.	23	2
201404	3830	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	Notwithstanding my own ongoing concerns about unusually low interest rates for this extended period of time, I do think the effectiveness and credibility of our forward guidance are going to demand some further clarity as the economy strengthens, particularly because paragraph 6 talks about how, “even after employment and inflation are near mandate-consistent levels,” we expect that “economic conditions may, for some time, warrant keeping the target federal funds rate below” its long-run level.	23	2
201404	3831	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	This guidance could create some communication challenges.	23	2
201404	3832	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	As President Plosser noted, there have been a range of views as to why a below-normal target might be necessary, and several explanations have emerged around fighting lingering headwinds, following a highly inertial policy rule, or responding to a lower long-term equilibrium funds rate.	23	2
201404	3833	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	If persistent headwinds are the reason for low rates, we should try, over time, to be more specific simply to ensure that we know what we are trying to offset by holding rates low.	23	2
201404	3834	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	The equity risk premium, a low housing share of GDP, and fiscal headwinds are some factors that have been noted in the past as weighing on the recovery.	23	2
201404	3835	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	As these factors move toward more normal levels or not, we can be prepared to reassess the stance of policy.	23	2
201404	3836	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	But if these headwinds are, in fact, subsiding and we’re instead moving our policy rate with considerable inertia or have a view that the longer-run neutral rate has fallen, we should be prepared to explain these other aspects of the policy to our market participants or risk sending confusing signals.	23	2
201404	3837	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	The second issue that I would just note briefly is one that was referenced yesterday around the reinvestment policy and how we talk about that.	23	2
201404	3838	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	I noted from the recent memo that compared the Tealbook assumptions with survey-based measures of market participants that dealer responses varied widely regarding expectations of cessation of reinvestments, from early 2015 to mid-2016.	23	2
201404	3839	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	So the signaling aspect of such cessation will likely be strong, and I think our groundwork that we might lay could help coalesce expectations around our intentions as we go forward.	23	2
201404	3840	2014-04-30	meeting	GEORGE	Thank you.	23	2

201404	3855	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The SEP suggested that, over the course of 2016, the federal funds rate will rise by considerably less than 25 basis points per meeting.	23	2

201404	3857	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	Will we raise the fed funds rate target by less than 25 basis points per meeting?	23	2

201404	3859	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	The concern I have about that is, even in normal times, having that degree of control over the fed funds rate might be challenging if we were trying to raise it by, say, 10 basis points a meeting.	23	2
201404	3860	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	From the conversation we had yesterday, I came away feeling that that might be even more of an issue as we exit this time around.	23	2

201404	3864	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	It’s more that we should be thinking ahead about the choice we prefer so that we can develop appropriate communication plans and, if necessary, enhance our technical ability to move whatever target rate we want in smaller-than-usual increments.	23	2
201404	3865	2014-04-30	meeting	KOCHERLAKOTA	As I suggested yesterday—and I like what President Plosser said here, too—we should also be planning for the contingency—I view this as remote—that we might want to raise our target interest rate very rapidly.	23	2

201404	3952	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	That would create a bit more clarity about where the weight of the FOMC was in terms of the dots.	23	2
201404	3953	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I do think another thing we can do, though, is something that the Chair did in her speech at the Economic Club of New York:  She emphasized the uncertainties that surround the forecast and therefore the uncertainties about how monetary policy will evolve as the forecast changes.	23	2
201404	3954	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	That’s a theme that we could provide greater emphasis to, because the fact is, the dots of the SEP have this perverse consequence of overemphasizing the modal forecasts in the markets’ minds, and then it feeds back into very low volatility that actually concerns us.	23	2
201404	3955	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	If we can get people away from the modal forecast a little bit so they understand that this is almost certainly not likely to be realized, that would be another way of addressing the dots problem.	23	2
201404	3956	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	As I said yesterday, we’ve got to get the reinvestments issue on the table and resolve that.	23	2
201404	3957	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	I guess I’m generally sympathetic with Governor Powell’s view that putting it after the liftoff in terms of short-term rates probably makes the most sense, just because we’re going to be quite nervous about what the liftoff itself is going to do with respect to financial conditions.	23	2
201404	3958	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Separating them by some period of time might be best, but I’m open to discussion on that.	23	2
201404	3959	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	Finally, as the Vice Chairman, I want to thank Sandy and Jeremy for all of their work and for being such good colleagues.	23	2
201404	3960	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	The word “collegial” is a word that would apply very, very, very well to both of you.	23	2
201404	3961	2014-04-30	meeting	DUDLEY	It’s been a pleasure to work with you both, and I and everyone else on the Committee wish you the best of luck in your new endeavors.	23	2
201404	3962	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Thank you.	23	2
201404	3963	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Okay.	23	2
201404	3964	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	Well, I think this has been a very good discussion, and we’ve heard many valuable suggestions for what we should be doing in coming meetings, particularly in terms of looking at reinvestment policy and how we’re going to communicate that along with our plans for normalization; what to do about the dot plot; and other matters, including possible ways to clarify the logic of the guidance in paragraph 6, which many of you have mentioned.	23	2
201404	3965	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	We’ll keep these in mind and try to put these on the agenda for future meetings.	23	2
201404	3966	2014-04-30	meeting	YELLEN	But, with respect to today’s decision, I heard widespread support for alternative B as indicated in the statement.	23	2
